Natalia Karayaneva, thank you so much. Thank you.

Stanford AI Club: Chamath on How to Win in the AI Era

Stanford AI Club(18.1K)·54:23·14.7K views·2026-05-05
Chamath PalihapitiyaCo-founder, Social Capital
TL;DRChamath Palihapitiya argues that the next phase of AI value creation lies in building a "control plane" that translates English-language business secrets and symbolic logic into deterministic code, moving beyond simple pattern matching to solve complex, long-horizon enterprise tasks.

TL;DR

Chamath Palihapitiya argues that the next phase of AI value creation lies in building a "control plane" that translates English-language business secrets and symbolic logic into deterministic code, moving beyond simple pattern matching to solve complex, long-horizon enterprise tasks.

The 8090 Thesis: Bridging the Symbolic and Embedded Spaces

  • The AI Maturity Gap: Current AI models are "fancy autocompletes" that predict tokens based on patterns rather than thinking from first principles. Consequently, they fail at complex problems and long-horizon tasks.
  • The Symbolic Control Plane: Palihapitiya’s company, 8090, aims to build a "honeypot" for the symbolic space—the English-language requirements, PRDs, and tribal knowledge that define a company’s success.
      • English as the Universal Code: English is the unifying language for eight billion people; innovation will shift from technical algorithm design to "judgment" and "ideas" expressed in plain language.
      • The Software Factory: 8090 uses a "software factory" model where humans collaborate with models to bind English requirements to engineering plans.
      • Legacy Modernization: This approach allows companies to reverse-engineer millions of lines of legacy code (e.g., COBOL) into English-language "secrets" that current executives can actually understand and manipulate.
  • Avoiding the Trough of Disillusionment: To justify the trillions of dollars being invested in AI, companies must prove a massive ROI through 50%+ productivity gains. This requires rebuilding how companies operate from the "studs" up.

Organizational Design: The "Infinite Game" and Radical Efficiency

  • Finite vs. Infinite Games: Palihapitiya defines success as moving from finite games (competing for scarce resources like promotions) to infinite games (practicing a craft to prove something to oneself).
  • The 8090 Operating Model:
      • Eliminating Hierarchy: The company operates without a traditional org chart to dismantle "traditional ways of thinking" and force camaraderie in the struggle.
      • Massive Under-hiring: By hiring 40 people to do 80 people’s worth of work, the organization forces employees to abandon arrogance and think from first principles.
      • The Flow State: Like SpaceX and OpenAI, the goal is to create high-pressure environments that strip away ego and focus purely on "what can we do better?"
  • The Recruitment Profile: He seeks individuals focused on their own "journey to resilience," preferring those who have no "priors or bias" from older enterprise software eras.

Future Trends: Distributed Compute and Network Effects

  • The Network Effect of Code: Palihapitiya envisions a future where disparate industries (e.g., a hospital and an aerospace company) share a library of underlying code patterns.
      • Cross-Industry Cooperation: By recompiling code through different "ontologies," the N-plus-first company can leverage the "secrets" of all companies that came before it, creating logarithmic abundance.
  • Open-Weight vs. Closed-Source: He highlights a critical distinction between "closed-source" (US) and "open-weight" (China) models.
      • The Danger of "Vassal States": If five entities control AI, everyone else becomes a "vassal state."
      • Distributed Infrastructure: He advocates for unregulated, distributed forms of compute and inference (e.g., BitTensor) to ensure AI has no "kill switch" and remains de-levered from government oversight.
  • The Positive-Sum Compact: Palihapitiya rejects AI doomerism and the narrative of replacing workers. He advocates for a "methodical" approach where AI helps humans do more, works within regulations, and increases the value of the workforce.

Leadership and Personal Resilience

  • The Fallacy of Failure: Palihapitiya views failure as a social construct used to keep people down. He frames it as "do and learn" rather than an identity.
  • Ego Management: He uses specific "guardrails" to maintain humility:
      • Orthogonal Partners: Surrounding himself with people (like his wife, a pharma CEO) who are unimpressed by ego and focused on practical, high-stakes work.
      • Poker as a Diagnostic: He views poker as a mirror for his mental state; losing streaks often indicate that ego is interfering with judgment.
  • Trauma as a Catalyst: He openly discusses how childhood adversity fueled his initial drive, but emphasizes that long-term success requires evolving past a "less than" mindset toward self-acceptance.
"The ultimate hack for AI is when things are in English. That is the unifying language for eight billion human beings. It is not C, it is not Python... and the space of innovation is not algorithms anymore. It's judgment." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"There is no failure. That is what other people think of you to keep you down. There is no failure... There is do and learn, do and learn, do and learn." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"AI says, 'I am going to learn, I'm going to tokenize that knowledge, and then I'm going to sell subscriptions... I'm going to use that to replace you and fire you.' That is not a positive-sum view... The positive-sum view is we're going to work together." — Chamath Palihapitiya
Thank you guys for coming. Today we have a very special guest who I think needs realistically no introduction, but we're going to introduce him regardless. Chamath is the founder and CEO of 8090, an AI-native company focused on rebuilding and modernizing enterprise solutions. Prior to 8090, Chamath was the founder and CEO of Social Capital, where he backed companies such as Slack, SoFi, Groq, and others. Before that, he was an early executive at Facebook where he scaled the company to the size it is today, and he is a co-host of the All-In Podcast. Chamath, it's a pleasure to have you here today. Thank you for taking the time to join us. Thanks. I went to school with his mom, and I just met his mom. It's all full circle. Small world. All right, so as a brief kind of overview of how the structure's going to go today, guys, we have some questions that we collected that you entered, and then we'll open up the floor to some audience questions. So just if you have any questions, note them down and be ready for them later. So, Jason, you want to kick it off with the first question? Yeah, sure. I guess, like, the first one, you've had a very unconventional career in tech. I think we'd all say, obviously, with the beginnings as an immigrant, and then through Facebook, VC, and then now to founding. Curious just for the broader audience: how did your early life shape the way you think about risk, ownership, and building? Okay, well, first of all, thank you for saying it this way because a lot of people sometimes wonder, like, what's the motivation? I think, like, Kobe Bryant actually had the best answer for this, which is the winning and the losing is sort of like a byproduct of what it feels like to be in the moment. And in the moment, you have the opportunity to practice a craft, get better at something, and mostly prove something to yourself. There are many other ways in which people say that. Some people say that it's how you play an infinite game. Like if you play a finite game, you and I are competing for the scarce resource of a promotion, or for venture capital, or for some external validation, some award, some title. And that it's finite, right? And that's a proposition where if I'm winning, you're losing, and vice-versa. The infinite game is more where you're just like—it's like this constant trajectory. What I've accidentally stumbled into is an infinite game, whereas where I started was playing finite games, where I was competing with everybody around me. So now if you ask me what my motivation is, I think that I've done a couple of really interesting things. When I was an executive, I was mostly asked by somebody else, in that case Zuck, to take on a task. And I had to be a trusted steward, but fundamentally it was his show and I was a supporting actor. And then I left, and then I started an investment business, which was a completely orthogonal thing. At Facebook, I was really successful, I think, and probably could have just stopped. But when I moved—when I went to Social Capital, I had to kind of find a way of betting on other people and managing the capital of other people. When you take money from other people, I don't know about how you guys would react to it, but it was profoundly stressful for me. The people that gave me money, like Mayo Clinic or the pension fund of XYZ, I felt so bad about this idea that I could lose money for these folks. And so I really took that job seriously and then I wanted to prove what it meant to take risk and be an investor. I did that job well. The great thing about 8090 is that it's my first real shot—I've started many projects, I've gotten them off the ground, I'll be an executive chairman—but it's my first real shot at doing the one thing that I've never done. I started something from scratch, the white paper, clean sheet design: "This is what I think we should do," and then build it. And I need to see if I can help make it successful. And if I do that, I feel like I will have done everything professionally that is a challenge. How do you build a trillion-dollar company? Check. How do you build a multi-billion dollar investor firm? Check. Can you start and scale and create something that's many billions? I don't know. But the reason I can be motivated is that I'm framing it as a challenge of who I am as a person. Long-winded answer, but it is such a key to your success. I wish I knew that when I was younger. So many times I blew myself up. So many times I self-sabotaged myself. So many times I just—I fucked up the core essence of what the opportunity was. And the problem with places like this is it can really trick you. I went to an equivalent place like this, not nearly as prestigious, but still quite prestigious in Canada, called the University of Waterloo. You're surrounded by the smartest kids. In that case it's Canadian for the most part, and from, you know, Hong Kong. That's what it was. Canadians and Hong Kong. But they're little geniuses. And when you're surrounded by geniuses, it's easy to get very outwardly focused and competitive with one another. And it is a huge distraction. So you were saying earlier that you shifted from this finite reward system to this infinite reward system where instead of competing with other people for finite resources, you're now on your own lane, if I'm understanding correctly. What were some of the key kind of realizations or practices? Like, how do you—how do you get to that stage? And I feel like a lot of us here grew up in very similar circumstances. You have to run into the wall really hard at high speed. Maybe there are people that are more evolved than I am. I think women are more evolved than maybe they can realize. Men are stupid, so you're going—you have to—you have to just really run into it face-first. And the reason is because one of the most shocking things that you can do is not have an org chart. We don't have an org chart. And so, you know, you drop these people into this soup. It's already stressful. That's one way. There's nobody to—so, you know, we have like nice, kind people that are also struggling. And so there's camaraderie in the struggle and they're willing to help you, but they have just as little of a fucking clue as you do. But that's—there's something beautiful in that, because now you, again, you have to just dismantle all of this traditional way of thinking. Two, you under-hire massively. If you have 80 humans' worth of work, having 40 is an incredibly interesting observational experiment. What happens? And now you combine it with this other pressure: there is no hierarchy to help you. What happens? And eventually what you see is all of these traditional things melt away. Traditional answers to questions melt away. "If this, then that," all melts away, and all of a sudden people are forced to just think from first principles. That is a beautiful place to be, especially early in your career. That's where the magic happens. You become a wholly different person. So I tell people, if you find one of these places—Anthropic is such a place, OpenAI is such a place, Facebook was such a place, Google was such a place, maybe there's pockets of that that are like that now—SpaceX is such a place. And you get into those places because it takes you into that flow state. There's so much work, there's so much pressure, but it strips away the traditional thinking. You have no time for arrogance. Like if you talk to Mark Juncosa at SpaceX, one of the most humble human beings, and you'd be like, "How the fuck is this guy?" and he's like, "What can we do better?" I was with somebody this weekend and we were just commenting on Mark. It's always the question: "What can I do better? What is SpaceX not doing well enough?" When you're a $200 billion company and the CTO asks those kinds of questions, it's a living embodiment of what it means to be in that flow state. But could you imagine the stuff that he is juggling, the pressure that he must be under? And so he has no time to be arrogant. That's what you want. You want to find places like that and you want to run to those places. They're incredibly challenging in some other ways, but they're incredibly unique. Just want to keep us on track. You mentioned 8090, this is obviously like a newer company of yours. What was kind of the rationale, like what was the catalyst of why you started 8090? What was something that you saw, some inefficiency that made you realize that "I'm not going to start another SaaS app" or something? So the way that my mind works is I always think in terms of is there a framework and a set of rules that allow me to abstract what is happening and give it a context. The most important context in AI is all the way back to how the internet initially developed. And the most important context of that is this thing called the OSI reference model. If you just Google that very quickly on your phone, what you're going to see is this image of a seven-layer cake. And at every layer, what's the most important thing to acknowledge is at every layer of that stack, you have seen hundreds of billions to trillions of dollars of value created. So even back then, when those people were imagining what the layers of abstraction would be, they got it so right. That thing was so valuable to me because I used to refer to that all the time when I would meet a new company and I was thinking, "Is this an interesting company?" I would go back to this OSI model and I would say, "Does this logically fit in any one of these things? Where does it fit? What will it do?" It wasn't the, you know, ultimate question, but it was a very important starting point. So I have a framework of that for AI. And in that framework, I spent the first number of years purely in the silicon. Helped this thing called Groq get off the ground. And initially how 8090 started was I thought that what we needed to do was essentially a transpiler, where you could take essentially CUDA and allow it to get to Trainium, Inferentia, AMD, all of this stuff. That's how it initially started. But it turned out to be wrong. I had spent like a bunch of years working and helping the team at Groq. Then I spent a handful of years completely pivoting and doing what I call the fulcrum assets of AI. What are those? It's prismatic LFP and it's actuation. Why? Because if you look inside of a digital AI stack, the most important thing that it lacks is power. The most critical thing for power is both the power generation and the power storage. The power storage, fundamentally iron—where's the OpenAI guy? I mean, where's the Anthropic guy? He was here. Iron, and then you'll see—it'll be prismatic LFP. So I went and helped start a bunch of things there. If you look at physical AI, the biggest Achilles' heel that people will have is the actual actuation. You know, how do you get the sensors to work? How do you kind of pass the "numb-match test" as they call it? How do you do that? The only answer for that is rare earths. And so I spent a bunch of time in that. Then when I came back, back to my reference model, I was looking around, started 8090, thought it was a transpilation problem, realized very quickly it was not. And then it kind of occurred: we have done so much—and I hate to offend anybody here when I say this—we have done so much in AI, okay? We have. But we are training models to understand patterns that we have seen. We do not think from first principles. It's token and then the prediction of the next token, prediction of the next token. I think that that's—it's still an incredible leap that we've created, or that OpenAI, Anthropic, xAI, that they have created. But what is missing? What's missing is being able to translate that in two dimensions. Dimension number one is long-horizon tasks are fundamentally still a joke. It doesn't work. And I don't care what anybody says, don't show me a stupid eval, don't tell me about some dumb script you ran for 48 hours. That's all bullshit. Long-horizon tasks are not well-handled. They just don't work. Second is complex problems also don't work. They are not well-addressed and they're not well-handled. Why is this important? If AI looks like any other technology, we're going to go through the rise, right, the initial hype cycle. We're going to see a natural contraction because somehow, somewhere, something is going to fail. We are all going to see this. And then that's what's called the trough of disillusionment, I think. The MBA folks will confirm if that's true. Then you see the slow, gradual uptake of the real final solution. Happened in the internet, happened before, it's happened in many, many, many cases. The problem with this is that we, though, are spending hundreds and hundreds of billions to trillions of dollars to try to figure out how to cross this chasm. So what do we do? If we don't figure this out, people will hit the trough of disillusionment and say this was a joke. I think we need to be able to take AI into very complicated environments and make it work. So what's my solution to that? I think at a very basic level, you have to have a symbolic space that guides the embedded space. So maybe a little less jargon-y: what is a secret of a company? Like what's the secret inside of Google? What's the secret inside of Uber? What's the secret inside of TikTok? What that is is a symbolic representation. It's when you're in a meeting and somebody says, "Well, you know, here's how we should do the PageRank this way. Here's how we should build GFS in Bigtable." It's all in the symbolic space of English. It's all these like secrets that a company has that describes what they should do. So I was thinking to myself, like, the most valuable thing we could do is build a massive honeypot for the symbolic space, for English. Like for the requirements, for all the secrets, for all of the—like when you guys go work inside a company, at some point, if your company does poorly, they'll bring in McKinsey. And then you'll spend tens of millions of dollars. And what will you get at the end? You'll get a deck, a nice slide deck. What do you do with the deck? Most of the time nothing. And that's the business model. The executives feel better. You'll buy some off-the-shelf piece of software, it will be sold to you as a solution. You'll try to implement it, it really won't work. Then you'll go to a consulting firm, they'll hire hundreds and hundreds of time-and-materials people that look like me—we'll try to implement it, it won't work. And this cruft builds up. So my idea is I want to build basically a control plane for AI. I want to be able to really focus on the things that are the symbolic golden source, the true source, the English-language understanding of what should be done, what is done, what works, what doesn't work. PRDs, requirements. Those are the hard-fought secrets that allow companies to be successful. Code is a mechanistically deterministic thing that either works or doesn't work. So you will get that to perfection well before you get requirements and understanding to perfection. So back to this original problem: when you put trillions of dollars in the ground and someone finally says, "What is the ROI?" you're going to have to point to the economy and say, "These companies are 50% more productive." Unless you completely rebuild how those companies operate, it will not pay that off and there will be blood in the streets. The stock market will just totally turn over on itself. So we have a responsibility to do that. So we have this thing that we call a software factory. You get all these humans that can collaborate with a bunch of very powerful models and you can take all these requirements and you can get them into a very amazing sense of what the scope of what you want to do is, and then we bind it to an engineering plan. You bind that to work orders. Now all of a sudden things can work in the forward pass, the reverse pass. We can take tens of billions of lines of—or tens of millions of lines of codebases, dump them in, and all of a sudden it just propagates backwards and now all of a sudden you have English-language understanding: "This is exactly what this does." And these companies, their eyes are like, "We didn't even know." We have this one customer who's got such an old legacy codebase, they have to go and get retirees to—I swear to God, dude, this is a hundred-billion-dollar-a-year company—out of retirement to come into the office to explain what this COBOL code does. And none of you geniuses were able to figure that out, and nobody before you was able to figure it out, and it turns out that these kinds of problems are the thing that's stopping ROI on trillions of dollars of investment. It's all the hidden context. Hidden context, tribal knowledge. So Peter Thiel calls it like—all great companies are built around secrets. And I think an interesting thought challenge is: what happens if all these secrets get documented? What happens if there's like—so you guys live in a world where GitHub is like the ground truth. Well, instead of GitHub, what if there was an actual set of wiki documents in plain language that the CEO could actually read, that were the actual secrets? You know, what if there was a document that Uber had that actually described surge pricing in English, not in code? And when you manipulate the English-language understanding of the rule, the actual surge pricing algorithm then changes downstream to hundreds of millions of people? What if that were possible? You start to unlock a level of optimization and improvement because you don't have to be technical anymore, you just have to have good judgment and be smart. There's way more people that have good judgment and are smart than are technical. So this is the whole point. Like when technology works, you're opening the aperture. Like when I was in college, I had to learn how to code in C and C++. And you're manipulating memory at this ultra-low level. And then at some point, PHP shows up, and then Python shows up, and JavaScript shows up, and you start to abstract up. And what happened? More of you knew how to do it. Many, many more of you, like Python, raise your hand? Okay, most of the room. C++ raise your hand? Oh bullshit, come on—stop that. Come on, stop. I'm trying to make a point here, guys. Unfortunately, the CS core Stanford class is C++. Okay, well that's smart because you need to understand memory and memory structures. So I get that. But for most people, the point I'm trying to make—you might get more people with C, or maybe assembly. Okay, how many of you know assembly? There's a few. Oh my god, Jesus Christ. But the point I'm trying to make is as the languages get abstracted, the N, the number of people that can participate in that ecosystem goes up. And so the ultimate hack for AI is when things are in English. That is the unifying language for eight billion human beings. It is not C, it is not Python, it is none of these languages here. It's this language here and the space of innovation are not algorithms anymore. It's judgment. It's really good ideas. And that many, many, many more people have. I think touching on this, there is an interesting analogy that said like when the first compiler was invented, there was a lot of kind of uncertainty around with this new tool, right? Like, you would have this code, you'd write it, and then pass it through the compiler, but you weren't sure that like the assembly that it generated was actually correct. And I think we're in like this kind of similar stage with AI, right? Like we have this grand vision, we want to reason on the symbolic level, but in order to do that we have to pass it through kind of this software factory as you're describing, and there are all these kind of like lossy conversions that can happen. And so you mentioned earlier as well, like these agents—there's so much hype around like, "Oh, like Open Claude running for 48 hours and solved my life" or something crazy like that. It's clearly not true, it's clearly an exaggeration and one of the big reasons of this is, as you were saying, there's a lot of secrets, right? There's landmines, you have to bring in the retired developer from 15 years ago to explain why something happened and explain why ChatGPT deleted this line and now the whole codebase is broken. So in this path towards this ultimate goal, right, we want to reason in English, what are some of the biggest challenges that you guys are facing that you think more people need to be thinking about? So one is a technical challenge and the other one is one of business. The technical challenge is we're struggling with the same things that everybody else struggles with: what is the interplay and the interface between where the agent starts and ends and where the humans get involved? How do you actually create the right evals and the right guardrails for the agents to stay on task? How do you actually create a knowledge graph that these agents can traverse so that they can stay at the level of embeddings and actually be able to act over reasonably long-horizon tasks?
